A P P E N D I X 11.

STATISTICAL TABLE

Definitions and explanatory notes.

The following general notes supplement the footnotes given below the

individual tables.-

1. In an attempt to bring the material up-to-date provisional figures

are included in some tables.

2. Figures in some tables have been rounded off to the nearest final digit. Hence there may be a slight discrepancy between the total

as shown and the sum of its components.

3. Diferences as compared with previously published figures are due

to subsequent revisions.

4- The following symbols have been used throughout.-

n.a. = not available.

— = nil.

negligible.

Estimates for 1968 to 1970 are provisional.

Item 1 (A) Goods at retail values.

Item I ( B ) Locally produced goods. Estimates of tea and coconut included in this item have been computed on the basis of data on per capita consumption; while the data used in computing the estimates for the years 1960 to 1962 have been obtained from the Consumer Finance Survey 1953, the data used from 1963 onwards have been obtained from the Consumer Finance Survey 1963. Thus, the estimates for 1964 onwards are strictly not comparable with those for earlier years.

Item IV (i) — Changes in stocks in the following items only - imported rice, wheat flour and sugar, GPS paddy and arrack.

Item V — Equals expenditure on consumption, gross domestic fixed capital formation and changes in stocks.

Item VI — Net investment abroad=the surplus to the nation on current account.

:; The accounts of the Central Bank of Ceylon for the year ended 31st December, 1970 have been audited under my dhection. Agricultural Credit S c h e m e -

The rate of interest on refinance loans granted to commercial banks on the pledge of usance promissory notes of Ca-operatives relating to the production of paddy, red onions, Bombay onions, chillies, potatoes, pineapples and vegetables is 14%-

Purchase of agricultural produce -

The rate of interest on refinance facilities to commercial banks on the security of promissory notes in respect of advances granted to Co-operatives to finance the purchase of paddy under the G.P S. and for the purchase, sale or storage of locally grown agricultural products is 3%.

Commercial and Production Loans;-

The rate of interest on advances against usance promissory notes relating to commercial and production loans is 64%

(c) These rates relate only to the larger commercial banks in Ceylon.

(d) From January 1970, the major banks pay 5 per cent on fixed deposits on amounts of Rs 25,000 and over fo; periods of 3-12 months. The rates on deposits for 2 4 , 36 6A 48 month deposits are 5 } , 5j snd 5 | pet cent respectively. Banks in Ceylon do not generally pay interest on current account deposits.

Note. The required reserves are computed weekly every Friday on the basts of deposit liabilities as on the previous Wednesday .Figures so computed are applicable until next Thursday. (1) Includes inter-bank deposits.

(5) Pursuant to regulations under sections 10 c, 93, 94, 96 ck 97 of the Monetary Law Ac t

(Chapter 422) commercial banks are required to maintain with the Central Bank cash reserves amounting to 12 per cent of their demand deposits and 5 per cent of their time and savings deposits. Banks are also required to maintain reserves amounting to 10 per cent of their special deposits accepted in terms of section 69 ( A ) (2) (b) of the Inland Revenue Act No. 4 of 1963, as amended by Act No. 18 of 1965. With effect from 10th February, 1961 commercial banks (except People's Bank) are required to maintain special reserves of 38 per cent against any increase in their demand deposits over the level of such deposits as at the close of business on February 1, 1961 With effect from 18th June, 1965 People's Bank is required to maintain special reserves of 28 per cent against any increase in demand deposits, above the level as at close of business on 9th June, 1965. Between September 18, 1953 and August 26, 1960, the 'statutory reserves' were 10 per cent of demand deposits and 5 per cent of time and saving deposits.

(7) From July 1951 commercial banks are permitted to maintain required reserves partly in the form of till cash and partly in the form of rupee deposits in the Central Bank.

(10) Total till cash up to July 1961. From July 1961, total till cash less amount set apart as reserves.

(a) According to Central Bank books.

(b) Figures based on deposit liabilities as on the last working day of the year,

t Reserve requirements were not enforced in view of the demonetisation

(f) Includes special loans granted by the Central Bank to the Government in terms of the Bretton Woods Agreement Act No. 20 of 1950 as amended by Section 4 of the Bretton Woods Agree­ment (Amendment) Act No. 19 of 1959,the International Development Association Agreement Act. No. 7 of 1961 and the Asian Development Bank Agreement (Ratification) Act No. 21 of 1966. The first special loan which amounted to Rs. 14.3 million was granted by the Central Bank to the Government in August, 1950 to meet its obligations to the I.B.R.D. The Govern­ment repaid the loan in full inMay, 1952 and converted Rs. 12.7 millionof its paid up subscriptions to the I.B.R.D. into a promissory note. The special loans were resumed in October, 1959. At the end of September, 1969 such special loans amounted to Rs. 89.5 million. (g) Loans from semi-government agencies. (i) areceipt of Rs. 28.9 million of P.L. 480 loans in the financial year 1967/68 was recorded only in 1968/69 due to delay in the receipt of information regarding disbursements. (H) U.S. P.L. 480,1 C.A. and A.I.D. loans since 1965/66, which were earlier regarded as project loans are now classified under non-project (commodity) loans.

N O T E : Under the Commodity Loan Agreements between the Government of Ceylon and the countries giving aid, commodities are made available to the Government of Ceylcn. Lo:al importers are allowed to import these commodities on depositing with the Central Bank, the Ceylon Rupee equivalent of the foreign exchange cost of these cenrrcdities. These deposits form a major portion of the "Ceylon Government Foreign Aid Counter, part Funds Account'. This account is also credited with-(a) proceeds of loans which are tied to projects but art received in the fotm of commodities,' and (b) proceeds of grants received in the form of commodities.

The Treasury periodically withdraws funds from this account to finance Government expenditure.

Note:—The figures in the above table differ from statistics on external assets published in the Annual Reports and Bulletins up to December 1961. The differences where they occur are explained more fully in the footnotes that follow. Th!.s revision of the data on external assets has been made for the following reasons. (1) The above sratistics are consistent with data published in the Annual Reports and Bulletins on inter­national reserves and foreign balances ot the Central Bank and commercial banks, respectively, as shown in the balance sheets of these institutions and in the statement of Ceylon's international transactions. (2) The above presentation conforms as far as possible to international practice as followed by the Inter­national Monetary Fund in their Balance oi Payments Year Books and International Financial Statistics. (3) The present series is more comprehensive than the hitherto published statistics as it includes balances due to Ceylon under payments agreements, export bills purchased and discounted by commercial banks and foreign currency notes and coins held by the banking system, all of which were not reflected in the previous presentation. (a) Includes War Loan re-lent to U . K . Government, less the patt held by the Currency Board / Central Bank. Figures upto 1952 agree with previously published statistics. Any difference thereafter is due to the inclusion of balances due to Cevloo under payments agreement with China. Except for these balances, which are record-ded according to local records, the basis of valuation throughout is at face value according to records abroad. (b) As previously published- i.e at face value according to records abroad. Includes foreign assets held by the Sterling Loan Sinking Funds. Ic) The assets of the Currency Board were transferred to the Central Bank on August 28, 1950, Figures upto 1949 are as previously published, based on local records at mid—market valuation. Thereafter the figures are as published under International Reserve in the balance sheet of the Central Bank (see Table 18)1. As explained in footnotes (a) to Table 18t the valuation of the International Reserve is according to local books at cost or face value, whichever is less. The International Reserve also includes balances due to Ceylon on bilateral clearing accounts other than with China. (d) All figures are according to local records, and upto 1947 are as previously published. From 1949 they agree with foreign balances of commercial banks as shown in Table 17. These balances are the sum of foreign currency on hand and balances due from banks abroad, and export bills purchased and discounted.

F O O T NOTES TO TABLE 32

(a) This table covers all the items of revenue and expenditure that enter into the computation

of the net cash surplus/deficit.

(b) The actual figures of revenue from 1961/62 onwards differ from the published figures in the Government Accounts due to the following adjustments:

(1) Inclusion of receipts from the National Development Tax viz: Rs. 18-6 million, Rs. 17.5 million, Rs. 2.9 million, Rs. 1.1 million, Rs. 0.5 million, Rs. 0.2 million and Rs.0.1 million in 1961/62, 1962/63, 1963/64,1964/65,1965/66, 1966/67 and 1967/68 respectively.

( 2 ) Exclusion of transfers to revenue of Rs. 20.6 million from the National Flood Relief Fun! in 1961/62, a book credit of Rs. 26.8 million from the Food Commissioner in 1965/66, a book transfer of Rs 44.2 million of profits from advance account activities in 1967/68 and a book credit of Rs. 38.7 million from the Food Commissioner in 1968/69 and

(3) Exclusion of the value of equipment and other aid gifted by foreign Governments and agencies viz Rs. 4.7 million, Rs. 2.8 million, Rs. 1.6 million, Rs. 1.5 million, Rs. 18.1 million, Rs. 0.1 million, Rs. 1.6 million, Rs.'O.l million andRs. 0.2 million in 1961/62, 1962/63, 1963/64, 1964/65, 1965/66, 1966/67, 1967/68, 1968/69 and 1969/70.

(c) For purposes of computing the net cash surplus/deficit, expenditure is defined to include recurrent expenditure, capital expenditure, expenditure chargeable to the National Develop, ment Reserve and the net receipt or payment on advance account activities. The difference between revenue and total expenditure thus defined gives the value of the net cash surplus/ deficit.

(d) Comprises the Railway Department, Electrial Department (until November, 1969 when it was converted into a corporation), Department of Posts and Telecommunications, Department of Information and Broadcasting (until January 5, 1967 when the Broadcasting Department was covetted into a corporation) and the Colombo Port Commission. The expenditure figures appearing in this table will not tally with the figures appearing in Table 35since the figures in Table 35 exclude items of a capital nature included under recurrent expenditure while the figures in this table include them.

(e) Excludes contributions from revenue to public debt sinking funds and direct repayments of public debt from revenue and special payments to International Financial Organisations-

(f) Excludes baok adjustments (debits and credits) arising from transfer to recurrent expenditure and capital expenditure of advances for loan works and losses on advance account activities incurred and financed in previous financial years- Hence the figures will not tally with the figures published in the Accounts of the Government of Ceylon.

(g) Includes expenditure chargeable to foreign loans, advances for loan works and also expen-diture met from foreign grants from 1960/61 onwards.

(h) From 1960/61 onwards the practice of classifying expenditure into "Expenditure Chargeable to Revenue" and "Expenditure Chargeable to Loan Funds" has been discontinued. Instead, the Government's total expenditure is divided into three major categories, viz. General Administrative Services, Social Services and Economic Services and Development. It is further classified into seven votes. Where it is difficult to identify the staff who are wholly engaged in the activities falling within the above categories, their emoluments are shown under General Administrative Services, vote 1. Rest of the expenditure under each of the categories is divided into recurrent expenditure, and capital expenditure, to form six votes. Thus recurrent expenditure consists of votes 1,2,4 and 6 and capital expenditure consists of votes 3,5 and 7. Since a similar classification is not available for the years prior to 1960/61, expenditure chargeable to revenue and expenditure chargeable to loan funds are assumed to be comparable to recurrent expenditure and capital expenditure respectively.

(i) Provisional.

(j) Original estimate-

1.01 Mainly receipts of the Departments of Agticultute, Irrigation, Examinations and other sundry Departments, hospital and dispensary receipts, sale of old stores and mineral deposits and other sundry receipts.

1.02 Rent of Government buildings and lands, revenue from crown forests and rest houses, Air Ceylon landing and housing charges and premia on leases etc.

1.03 Net profit of the Local Loans and Development Fund and profit from the sale of non-monetary gold by the Central Bank.

1.09 Mainly the surplus of the National Lottery.

1.10.1 Turnover tax only.

1.10.2 Duty on country liquor, tobacco tax, tea tax, betting tax and lottery tax.

1.10.3 Licence fees on imports are also included.

1.10.7 Mainly liquor licence fees, tavern rents, banderol duty on matches, radio licence fees, and in previous years the tax on heavy oil motor vehicles and the registration of professions and businesses tax.

1.10.8 Mainly stamps, including compositon duties on bank cheques and share certificates.

1.10.9 Entirely the Profit from the sale of arrack.

1.10.10 Profits from the purchase and sale of sugar, flour and certain other s"*" food stuffs. Also includes the rice subsidy tax.

1.11 Recoveries from local bodies for water supplied and reimbursement of expenses incurred by Central Government in the working of the Municipal Magistrates' Courts.

1.13 Mainly the Post Office Savings Bank, the Ceylon Savings Bank

and the Loan Board.

2.02.2 Wealth tax, gifts tax and in previous years the land tax. Also includes the capital levy in 1970/71.

2.03 Grants received from the U.N. agencies, C-Plan and other countries.

2.04.3 Mainly the Local Loans and Development Fund and the National Housing Fund. -

2.05.3 Mainly the Ceylon Savings Bank, the Post Office Savings Bank the Savings Certificates Fund, the Employees' Provident Fund, other provident and trust funds, the Insurance Corporation, insurance companies and departmental and other official funds.

2.05.4 Mainly the unspent balances of State corporations, sundry deposits, miscellaneous funds and administrative borrowings from Government agencies and the U. S. Disbursing Officers' Funds,

2.05.5 Comprises project and non-project (commodity) loans.

4.01.1(b) Capital expenditure on defence is also included here; •

4.01.2 (d) Comprises the expenditure of the Departments of Labour, Social Services and Probation and Child Care Services.

4.01.2 (e) Comprises the expenditure of the Zoological Gardens, the Kandyan Peasantry Rehabilitation Scheme, the National Archives, and the Departments of Town and Country Planning, of Wild Life, of Cultural Affairs and of Mosques and Muslim Charitable Trusts.

4.01.4 Comprises the Railway, Port Commission, the Department of Posts and Telecommunications, upto November 1969, the Electrical Department and up to the financial year 1966/67, the Broadcasting Department.

4.01.5 Mainly miscellaneous services rendered by the Railway to other Departments,

4.02.1 (a) Mainly food-subsidies, assistance to cultivators for purchase of fertilizers, seed paddy, payment of crop insurance premia etc.

4.02.1(d) Mainly financial assistance to tuberculosis patients, mid-day meals to school children, scholarships, bursaries, relief of distress on account of crop failure, storms, floods, drought etc., and in 1969/70 and 1970/71 "reimbursement to the Central Bank of monies spent by it in the discharge of its Credit Guarantee". 4.02.2 (a) (iii)

Mainly the tea export duty rebate, grants to assisted schools and y" pirivenas, ayurvedic institutions, Law Society, homes for the aged, cultural associations etc.

4.02.2 (b) Contributions to international organizations and scholarships tenable outside Ceylon.

5.01.3 (a) (b) and (c)

Grants to the River Valleys Development Board and the State Plantation Corporation and contributions to the capital of the Fisheries Corporation and State industrial corporations are included as capital transfers under tem 5.02.1 (b), in the "Public Finance" section of the text. 6. Total of items 1.2.4 to 1.2.7 of Table 30 with the following adjust­

ments. The 'net food subsidy' as appearing in Government Accounts each year differs from the figures of the net food subsidy provided by the Food Commissioner. Since the profits from food sales and the gross food subsidy that are used in this table are according to the Food Commissioner's data, the excess or short fall in the Food Commissioner's net figure over that in the Government Accounts will appear as a receipt or a payment under this item.
